<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Capsella rubella genome and the genomic consequences of rapid mating system evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2013 45:7"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-06-09"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Stephen Wright, Detlef Weigel and colleagues report the whole-genome sequence of Capsella rubella, a highly selfing crucifer found throughout much of southern and western Europe. They compare mixed-stage flower bud transcriptomes from C. rubella and C. grandiflora, finding a shift in expression of genes associated with flowering phenotypes and providing insights into the transition to selfing. The shift from outcrossing to selfing is common in flowering plants1,2, but the genomic consequences and the speed at which they emerge remain poorly understood. An excellent model for understanding the evolution of self fertilization is provided by Capsella rubella, which became self compatible &lt;200,000 years ago. We report a C. rubella reference genome sequence and compare RNA expression and polymorphism patterns between C. rubella and its outcrossing progenitor Capsella grandiflora. We found a clear shift in the expression of genes associated with flowering phenotypes, similar to that seen in Arabidopsis, in which self fertilization evolved about 1 million years ago. Comparisons of the two Capsella species showed evidence of rapid genome-wide relaxation of purifying selection in C. rubella without a concomitant change in transposable element abundance. Overall we document that the transition to selfing may be typified by parallel shifts in gene expression, along with a measurable reduction of purifying selection."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Stephen Wright, Detlef Weigel and colleagues report the whole-genome sequence of Capsella rubella, a highly selfing crucifer found throughout much of southern and western Europe. They compare mixed-stage flower bud transcriptomes from C. rubella and C. grandiflora, finding a shift in expression of genes associated with flowering phenotypes and providing insights into the transition to selfing."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The shift from outcrossing to selfing is common in flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Barrett, S.C.H. The evolution of plant sexual diversity. Nat. Rev. Genet. 3, 274–284 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1139">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Stebbins, G.L. Self fertilization and population variability in the higher plants. Am. Nat. 91, 337–354 (1957)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1142">2</a></sup>, but the genomic consequences and the speed at which they emerge remain poorly understood. An excellent model for understanding the evolution of self fertilization is provided by <i>Capsella rubella</i>, which became self compatible &lt;200,000 years ago. We report a <i>C. rubella</i> reference genome sequence and compare RNA expression and polymorphism patterns between <i>C. rubella</i> and its outcrossing progenitor <i>Capsella grandiflora</i>. We found a clear shift in the expression of genes associated with flowering phenotypes, similar to that seen in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, in which self fertilization evolved about 1 million years ago. Comparisons of the two <i>Capsella</i> species showed evidence of rapid genome-wide relaxation of purifying selection in <i>C. rubella</i> without a concomitant change in transposable element abundance. Overall we document that the transition to selfing may be typified by parallel shifts in gene expression, along with a measurable reduction of purifying selection.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The switch from obligatory outcrossing to predominant self fertilization in plants is one of the most striking and repeated examples of convergent evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Barrett, S.C.H. The evolution of plant sexual diversity. Nat. Rev. Genet. 3, 274–284 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1194">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Stebbins, G.L. Self fertilization and population variability in the higher plants. Am. Nat. 91, 337–354 (1957)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1197">2</a></sup>. Selfing is thought to be favored because of its inherent transmission advantage, as well as the advantage of assured reproduction when mates, pollinators or both are scarce. Selfing should evolve whenever these advantages outweigh the costs associated with inbreeding depression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Charlesworth, D. &amp; Willis, J.H. The genetics of inbreeding depression. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 783–796 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1201">3</a></sup>. In contrast to the immediate benefits of selfing, reduced effective recombination rates, greater population subdivision and more frequent genetic bottlenecks may incur longer-term costs as a result of reductions in effective population size and selective interference among linked sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth, D. &amp; Wright, S.I. Breeding systems and genome evolution. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 11, 685–690 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1205">4</a></sup>, all of which are potential contributors to the high rates of extinction of selfing lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lynch, M., Conery, J. &amp; Burger, R. Mutational meltdowns in sexual populations. Evolution 49, 1067–1080 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1209">5</a></sup>. A key problem in understanding the causes and consequences of the evolution of selfing has been partitioning the changes that occurred after the mating system evolution, as many species diverged before the evolution of selfing. As an example, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> probably became selfing only several million years after it was established as a separate species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1217">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tang, C. et al. The evolution of selfing in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 317, 1070–1072 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1220">7</a></sup>.</p><p>A unique opportunity to understand the evolution of selfing is offered by the genus <i>Capsella</i>, which is from the same family as <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The highly selfing species <i>C. rubella</i>, found throughout much of southern and western Europe, separated less than 200,000 years ago from the self-incompatible, obligate outcrosser <i>C. grandiflora</i>, which is restricted primarily to the northwest of Greece<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Guo, Y.L. et al. Recent speciation of Capsella rubella from Capsella grandiflora, associated with loss of self-incompatibility and an extreme bottleneck. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5246–5251 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1239">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Foxe, J.P. et al. Recent speciation associated with the evolution of selfing in Capsella. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5241–5245 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1242">9</a></sup>. In contrast to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, the breakdown of self incompatibility in <i>Capsella</i> was concurrent with species divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Guo, Y.L. et al. Recent speciation of Capsella rubella from Capsella grandiflora, associated with loss of self-incompatibility and an extreme bottleneck. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5246–5251 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1253">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Foxe, J.P. et al. Recent speciation associated with the evolution of selfing in Capsella. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5241–5245 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1256">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="St Onge, K.R., Kallman, T., Slotte, T., Lascoux, M. &amp; Palme, A.E. Contrasting demographic history and population structure in Capsella rubella and Capsella grandiflora, two closely related species with different mating systems. Mol. Ecol. 20, 3306–3320 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1259">10</a></sup>.</p><p>We shotgun sequenced the genome of the <i>C. rubella</i> reference line Monte Gargano (Italy) to 22× coverage using a combination of platforms (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec2">Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). For the final assembly of 134.8 Mb, covering all eight chromosomes, we used a genetic map with 768 markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Slotte, T., Hazzouri, K.M., Stern, D., Andolfatto, P. &amp; Wright, S.I. Genetic architecture and adaptive significance of the selfing syndrome in Capsella. Evolution 66, 1360–1374 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1278">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Tables 2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>), <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i> synteny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>) and BAC and fosmid paired-end link support (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). We predicted 28,447 transcripts from 26,521 protein-coding genes and 86 microRNA loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Tab1">Table 1</a> and Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec2">Methods</a>). We also conducted <i>de novo</i> genome assemblies from Illumina libraries for an outbred <i>C. grandiflora</i> accession and the close outgroup species <i>Neslia paniculata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Tables 6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Annotation results</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2669/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although the <i>C. rubella</i> genome assembly is <span class="stix">∼</span>40% shorter than the nuclear DNA content estimated from flow cytometry, 219-Mb, <i>k</i>-mer analysis and remapping of Illumina reads indicated that the assembly encompasses most of the euchromatin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Almost half of the 219-Mb genome seems to be repetitive, including centromeric satellite repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Apart from the centromeres, fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) identified the 45S ribosomal DNA (rDNA) arrays on chromosomes 2, 3 and 7, the 5S rDNA locus on chromosome 5 and an interstitial telomeric sequence in the pericentromeric region of chromosome 7 as notable genomic locations of repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig1">Fig. 1a,b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic structures, chromosome painting and comparative genomic mapping in C. rubella, A. lyrata and A. thaliana."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genomic structures, chromosome painting and comparative genomic mapping in <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="791"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparative genome structure and major chromosome landmarks in <i>C. rubella</i> (CR). The 24 ancestral genomic blocks are indicated by uppercase letters (A–X) and are colored according to their position on the eight chromosomes of the ancestral crucifer karyotype (ACK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1527">12</a></sup>). (<b>b</b>) Comparative chromosome painting of CR1 and CR2. Differentially labeled <i>A. thaliana</i> BAC contigs corresponding to the genomic blocks A, B, C, D and E were used as painting probes on the pachytene bivalents of CR1 and CR2. The true fluorescence signals were pseudocolored according to the color code used in <b>a</b>. The arrowheads indicate unpainted pericentromeric heterochromatin. Scale bars, 10 μm. (<b>c</b>,<b>d</b>) Comparative genome mapping of <i>C. rubella</i> with <i>A. lyrata</i> (AL; <b>c</b>) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (AT; <b>d</b>). The outer ring shows the percentage of the genomic window that comprises transposable elements, with a maximum at 60% coverage, the second ring shows gene density, and the inner ring shows orthologous regions between species on the basis of whole-genome alignment and orthologous chaining. Note that the <i>A. lyrata</i>, but not the <i>C. rubella</i>, assembly includes gaps for inferred centromeric heterochromatin. From synteny analyses of the three species, the approximate gene intervals contained within each block include: A/B, AT1G01010–AT1G36980; C, AT1G41830–AT1G56200; D, AT1G56210–AT1G64720; E, AT1G64790–AT1G80950; F, AT3G01070–AT3G25530; G, AT2G04039–AT2G07050; H, AT2G10870–AT2G20900; I, AT2G20920–AT2G26430; J, AT2G26670–AT2G48160; K, AT2G01060–AT2G04038; L, AT3G25545–AT3G32980; M/N, AT3G42170–AT3G63490; O, AT4G00026–AT4G05530; P, AT4G06534–AT4G12590; Q/R, AT5G01010–AT5G30510; S, AT5G32440–AT5G42110; T/U, AT4G12640–AT4G40100; V, AT5G42140–AT5G47760; W/X, AT5G47800–AT5G67640.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Consistent with previous findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1581">12</a></sup>, we found that the large-scale synteny between <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> is almost complete (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). Comparisons with <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>) indicated that all three major differences between <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> are either specific to the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome or errors in the <i>A. lyrata</i> assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). Further comparisons delimited the breakpoints of major rearrangements in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1626">12</a></sup>. Overall, we conclude that <i>C. rubella</i>, despite having gone through an extreme genetic bottleneck, retains a largely ancestral genome structure.</p><p>To investigate the functional consequences of the mating system change, we compared flower transcriptomes from four <i>C. rubella</i> and four <i>C. grandiflora</i> accessions. Many <i>C. rubella</i> alleles are found in <i>C. grandiflora</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Guo, Y.L. et al. Recent speciation of Capsella rubella from Capsella grandiflora, associated with loss of self-incompatibility and an extreme bottleneck. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5246–5251 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1647">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Foxe, J.P. et al. Recent speciation associated with the evolution of selfing in Capsella. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5241–5245 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1650">9</a></sup>; thus, DNA sequence variation should not confound RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) comparisons. RNA expression levels were more highly correlated within (average Pearson correlation coefficients, 0.95 for <i>C. grandiflora</i> and 0.94 for <i>C. rubella</i>) than between species (average Pearson correlation coefficient, 0.82; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). We identified 246 genes that were expressed more strongly in <i>C. rubella</i> relative to <i>C. grandiflora</i> and 373 that were expressed more weakly relative to <i>C. grandiflora</i>, with a minimum fold change of 1.5, false discovery and significance thresholds of 0.5% and a minimum normalized expression of 19 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). The set was enriched for Gene Ontology terms related to floral development and growth functions, which is consistent with changes in reproductive organ size and development between species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 11</a>). One-hundred fifty-eight differentially expressed genes colocalized with interspecific quantitative trait loci that are responsible for differences in petal size and pollen number<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Slotte, T., Hazzouri, K.M., Stern, D., Andolfatto, P. &amp; Wright, S.I. Genetic architecture and adaptive significance of the selfing syndrome in Capsella. Evolution 66, 1360–1374 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1680">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 12</a>), with 17 found within 2 Mb of petal size quantitative trait loci peaks. Thus, some of the expression changes may be due to <i>cis</i>-regulatory changes that had a role in floral evolution. Pathway analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>) identified a reduction in brassinosteroid signaling, which is involved in hormone-triggered pollen maturation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ye, Q. et al. Brassinosteroids control male fertility by regulating the expression of key genes involved in Arabidopsis anther and pollen development. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 6100–6105 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1693">13</a></sup>, in <i>C. rubella</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 13</a>).</p><p>To investigate whether the <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>C. grandiflora</i> pair is representative of the expression differences in flowers of closely related selfers and outcrossers, we compared the selfer <i>A. thaliana</i> and the predominantly outcrossing <i>A. lyrata</i>. We found that the overlap in expression changes between the two species pairs was much higher than that expected by chance. For example, of 373 genes that were expressed more strongly in <i>C. rubella</i>, 75 orthologs were also expressed more strongly in <i>A. thaliana</i> compared to <i>A. lyrata</i>, whereas only 16 showed higher expression in <i>A. lyrata</i>. In contrast, of 246 genes that were expressed more weakly in <i>C. rubella</i>, 46 orthologs were also expressed more weakly in <i>A. thaliana</i>, whereas only 12 showed lower expression in <i>A. lyrata</i> (Fisher's exact test <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−13</sup>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). These results suggest that parallel floral evolution in selfers may be associated with parallel changes in gene expression. A caveat is that some of these changes could reflect the altered abundance of specific tissue types because of changes in flower morphology.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Evolution of gene expression in selfing and outcrossing Capsella and comparisons to Arabidopsis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Evolution of gene expression in selfing and outcrossing <i>Capsella</i> and comparisons to <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="675" height="498"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Distribution of fold changes in gene expression in <i>C. grandiflora</i> relative to <i>C. rubella</i> (<i>x</i> axis) and <i>A. lyrata</i> relative to <i>A. thaliana</i> (<i>y</i> axis) at genes showing significant downregulation or significant upregulation in <i>C. rubella</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Population genetic theory predicts that selfers should accumulate slightly deleterious mutations because of a reduced effective population size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Charlesworth, D. &amp; Willis, J.H. The genetics of inbreeding depression. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 783–796 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1802">3</a></sup>. To test this hypothesis, we characterized genome-wide patterns of coding sequence polymorphisms discovered in RNA-seq data from five outbred <i>C. grandiflora</i> (ten haploid chromosomes) and six <i>C. rubella</i> individuals (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 14</a>). We identified 48,518 high-quality SNPs in 4,225 genes. The vast majority (81%) segregated only in <i>C. grandiflora</i>. Of the remainder, 7% segregated in both species, 8% segregated only in <i>C. rubella</i> and only 4% were fixed between the two species. On average, diversity at synonymous sites was 0.02 in <i>C. grandiflora</i>, whereas it was sixfold lower (0.003) in <i>C. rubella</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), which is similar to previously documented differences in smaller gene sets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="St Onge, K.R., Kallman, T., Slotte, T., Lascoux, M. &amp; Palme, A.E. Contrasting demographic history and population structure in Capsella rubella and Capsella grandiflora, two closely related species with different mating systems. Mol. Ecol. 20, 3306–3320 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1834">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Slotte, T., Hazzouri, K.M., Stern, D., Andolfatto, P. &amp; Wright, S.I. Genetic architecture and adaptive significance of the selfing syndrome in Capsella. Evolution 66, 1360–1374 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1837">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1840">12</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Polymorphism comparisons in C. rubella and C. grandiflora."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Polymorphism comparisons in <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>C. grandiflora</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="269"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Average pairwise differences (<i>π</i>) at nonsynonymous (nonsyn) and synonymous (syn) sites. Error bars indicate standard errors across all loci. Cr, <i>C. rubella</i>; Cg, <i>C. grandiflora</i>. (<b>b</b>) Ratio of nonsynonymous to synonymous polymorphisms at each derived frequency class using data subsampled to six chromosomes per species. <i>N. paniculata</i> was used as an outgroup to infer derived status. (<b>c</b>) Proportion of synonymous and nonsynonymous polymorphisms unique to each species, as well as shared and fixed differences. Simulated (sim) values are from forward computer simulations using the inferred demographic model and strength of selection on nonsynonymous sites (see main text). Obs, observed.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To test for a change in the efficacy of selection, we examined the ratios of nonsynonymous to synonymous polymorphisms. The ratio was much higher for <i>C. rubella</i>–specific (0.68) than <i>C. grandiflora</i>–specific SNPs (0.35; two-tailed Fisher's exact test <i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). This was true for all site frequency classes when subsampling the data to equivalent haploid sample sizes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), suggesting that relaxed selection is not simply due to rare variants in <i>C. rubella</i> having experienced less purifying selection after the recent genetic bottleneck that purged the majority of variation. In contrast, fixed SNPs behaved similarly to <i>C. grandiflora</i>–only SNPs, with a nonsynonymous-to-synonymous ratio of 0.38, whereas SNPs segregating in both species had the lowest ratio (0.22), consistent with these being the oldest on average and thus having experienced the most selection.</p><p>There are at least three potential explanations for the elevated nonsynonymous-to-synonymous ratio in <i>C. rubella</i>. The first is experimental error: because diversity is generally lower in <i>C</i>. <i>rubella</i>, SNP errors relative to true polymorphisms may inflate the relative measure of nonsynonymous variation. However, dideoxy sequencing indicated that the false positive rate was less than 2 × 10<sup>−3</sup> per SNP, which is much lower than the <i>C. rubella</i> polymorphism density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). Another explanation could be that the distribution of selection coefficients is altered because the evolution of selfing in <i>C. rubella</i> is associated with changes in morphology, life history, habitat and range. However, extensive population genetic modeling and computer simulations indicated that such a scenario is not required to explain the data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Fig. 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Tables 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">16</a>). The elevated nonsynonymous-to-synonymous ratio in <i>C. rubella</i> could also be due to a reduced efficacy of purifying selection caused by the demographic and selective effects associated with the shift to selfing, as is predicted by theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth, D. &amp; Wright, S.I. Breeding systems and genome evolution. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 11, 685–690 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1959">4</a></sup>. Our results seem to be the most consistent with this third hypothesis. Most previous work has found little or no evidence for relaxed selection on nonsynonymous sites in selfing lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Escobar, J.S. et al. An integrative test of the dead-end hypothesis of selfing evolution in Triticeae (Poaceae). Evolution 64, 2855–2872 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1963">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Haudry, A. et al. Mating system and recombination affect molecular evolution in four Triticeae species. Genet. Res. (Camb.) 90, 97–109 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1966">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wright, S.I., Lauga, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Rates and patterns of molecular evolution in inbred and outbred Arabidopsis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 1407–1420 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1969">16</a></sup>; our use of genome-wide polymorphism data to quantify current selection pressures in a recently derived selfing lineage provides a more powerful test of this hypothesis, suggesting that selfing lineages may in fact experience considerable accumulation of deleterious mutations even over short timescales.</p><p>Apart from genome-wide changes in the nonsynonymous-to-synonymous ratio, a potential consequence of the transition to selfing is a change in the abundance and distribution of transposable elements. Not only are self-replicating transposable elements expected to spread more efficiently through the genomes of highly outcrossing species, but self-regulated transposition is also more probable in selfers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wright, S.I. &amp; Schoen, D.J. Transposon dynamics and the breeding system. Genetica 107, 139–148 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1977">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Langley, C.H. The evolution of self-regulated transposition of transposable elements. Genetics 112, 359–383 (1986)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1980">18</a></sup>. In agreement, many transposable element classes have fewer members in the <i>A. thaliana</i> than in the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47183e1990">19</a></sup>. Despite its nuclear DNA content being in the same range as that of <i>A. lyrata</i>, the <i>C. rubella</i> genome is more similar to that of <i>A. thaliana</i> in several features related to transposable element frequency and density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). First, intergenic distances are more similar in <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Across all chromosomal blocks, the <i>A. lyrata</i>–to–<i>C. rubella</i> ratios were positive, with a mean of 1.6, whereas the mean for <i>A. thaliana</i> compared to <i>C. rubella</i> was 0.95. Thus, intergenic space has either shrunk in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> or has expanded in <i>A. lyrata</i>. Similarly, transposable element density is low in the <i>C. rubella</i> assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), particularly in gene-rich regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), and is more comparable to that in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). This suggests that genome expansion and contraction have occurred in different regions, with <i>C. rubella</i> having a compact euchromatic region comparable to <i>A. thaliana</i>, whereas <i>A. lyrata</i> has experienced greater recent transposable element activity near genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evolution of genome structure and transposable element abundance in Capsella and Arabidopsis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Evolution of genome structure and transposable element abundance in <i>Capsella</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="145"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Slope of the physical positions in orthologous blocks between <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>. Ancestral orthologous blocks are labeled on the <i>x</i> axis (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). (<b>b</b>) Transposable element genomic coverage in the three species. LINE, long interspersed nucleotide repetitive elements; SINE, short interspersed nucleotide repetitive elements. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of the distances between transposable elements and their nearest protein-coding genes. (<b>d</b>) Age distribution of full-length LTR retrotransposons estimated using the rate of substitution between LTRs of individual insertions and assuming a substitution rate of 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> per bp per generation. MYA, million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given that the structure of gene-rich regions in the <i>C. rubella</i> genome is similar to that in <i>A. thaliana</i>, we tested whether the shift to selfing was associated with a rapid loss of transposable element abundance, activity or both. The age distribution of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons in <i>C. rubella</i> seemed to be similar to that in <i>A. thaliana</i>, with no evidence for the high rate of recent transposition seen in <i>A. lyrata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>). Only 5% of the full-length LTR retrotransposons seemed to be younger than 100,000 years, which is close to the estimated speciation time (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 10</a>), suggesting that the vast majority of transposition occurred before the shift to selfing. However, identification of transposable element insertions using paired-end genomic Illumina sequencing revealed no clear evidence for consistent copy number differences between <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>C. grandiflora</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>). Similarly, although expression comparisons indicate a possible higher variance in transposable element expression in <i>C. rubella,</i> there is no evidence for consistently higher transposable element expression in <i>C. grandiflora</i> (Wilcoxon rank sum test <i>P</i> = 0.4857; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). In addition, transposable element density along the chromosomes is very similar in the two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>). Given that species divergence is low relative to the coalescent history of <i>C. grandiflora</i> (that is, most of the <i>C. rubella</i> alleles are shared with <i>C. grandiflora</i>), it is probable that longer timescales are required for transposable element copy number to diverge noticeably. Thus, our analyses suggest little evidence for large-scale changes in transposable element abundance, as the evolution of selfing in <i>Capsella</i> occurred about 100,000 years ago, and imply that transposable element activity may be specifically elevated in <i>A. lyrata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hollister, J.D. et al. Transposable elements and small RNAs contribute to gene expression divergence between Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 2322–2327 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2189">20</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Estimates of transposable element copy number and expression in C. rubella and C. grandiflora."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Estimates of transposable element copy number and expression in <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>C. grandiflora</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2669/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2669_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="131"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Numbers of insertion sites identified using read mapping of paired-end Illumina genomic data (using Popoolation TE) in two <i>C. rubella</i> accessions and two <i>C. grandiflora</i> accessions. SINE/LINE, SINE and LINE. (<b>b</b>) Mean and standard error of the proportion of RNA-seq transcripts mapping to transposable elements. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of transposable element insertions along chromosome 1 in two <i>C. rubella</i> accessions and two <i>C. grandiflora</i> accessions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2669/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>C. rubella</i> is a young species with an origin that is probably associated with a severe founder event and a shift to a highly selfing mating system. These recent and correlated events have had genome-wide consequences that range from divergence in gene expression for a suite of reproductively related genes to a genome-wide decline in the efficacy of natural selection on amino acid polymorphisms. Moreover, our comparisons among three closely related species, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>C. rubella</i>, highlight the fluidity of large-scale genome structure, typified by differential expansion of centromeric repeats and changes in transposable element activity. The factors driving such contrasting modes of genome expansion and shrinkage are far from resolved, and it will be important to broaden future comparisons to larger phylogenetic scales to better understand the processes driving genome structure evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation.</h3><p><i>Sequencing</i>. Whole-genome shotgun sequencing of <i>C. rubella</i> was conducted using the Monte Gargano (Italy) reference strain. The majority of the sequencing reads were collected with standard Sanger sequencing protocols and Roche 454 XLR and Illumina GAIIx machines at the DoE JGI in Walnut Creek, California (JGI sequencing protocols; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec7">URLs</a>). One linear Roche 454 library (ten runs, 2.78 Gb), three 2.5-kb insert size paired libraries (three runs, 713.1 Mb), two 4-kb insert size paired libraries (four runs, 434.5 Mb), one 8-kb insert size library (three runs, 935.4 Mb) and one 10-kb insert size library (two runs, 515.4 Mb) were sequenced with standard XLR protocols.</p><p><i>Genome assembly and construction of pseudomolecule chromosomes</i>. The sequence reads were assembled using our modified version of Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jaffe, D.B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2280">21</a>). This produced 1,859 scaffold sequences, with a scaffold L50 value of 6.7 Mb, 71 scaffolds larger than 100 kb and a total genome size of 137.1 Mb. Scaffolds were screened against bacterial proteins, organelle sequences and the GenBank nucleotide repository (nr) and removed if they were found to be contaminants. Additional scaffolds were removed if they (i) consisted of &gt;95% 24-mers that occurred four other times in scaffolds larger than 50 kb, (ii) contained only unanchored RNA sequences or (iii) were less than 1 kb in length.</p><p>The combination of 768 markers in an Illumina-based genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>), <i>A. lyrata</i> synteny derived from whole-genome alignments (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec2">Methods</a>) and BAC and fosmid paired-end link support was used to identify misjoins in the assembly. Scaffolds were broken if they contained syntenic and linkage group discontiguity coincident with an area of low BAC and fosmid coverage. To avoid bias, discontiguity on the basis of synteny alone was not deemed sufficient for breaking scaffolds. A total of 16 breaks were executed, and 45 broken scaffolds were oriented, ordered and joined using 37 joins to form the final assembly containing eight pseudomolecule chromosomes. The final assembly contained 853 scaffolds (9,675 contigs) that covered 134.8 Mb of the genome with a contig L50 value of 134.1 kb and a scaffold L50 value of 15.1 Mb. Except for a single potential misplacement of a small chromosome 7 contig on chromosome 4, the assembly was supported by an independent map with 999 markers within <i>C. rubella</i>, with 194 F2 individuals from the cross of 1408 and Monte Gargano<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Guo, Y.L., Todesco, M., Hagmann, J., Das, S. &amp; Weigel, D. Independent FLC mutations as causes of flowering time variation in Arabidopsis thaliana and Capsella rubella. Genetics 192, 729–739 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2299">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Completeness of the euchromatic portion of the genome assembly was assessed using 1,167 full-length cDNAs from <i>C. rubella</i> along with an 108-bp Illumina EST library from <i>C. grandiflora</i> leaf material (plants grown at the University of Toronto and library construction and sequencing conducted at the Genome Quebec Innovation Centre in Montreal). The aim of this analysis was to obtain a measure of the completeness of the assembly rather than a comprehensive examination of gene space. The screened alignments indicated that 1,166 of 1,167 (99.74%) of the full-length cDNAs aligned to the assembly, and 27,876 of 32,766 (86.29%) of the Illumina ESTs aligned. The ESTs that did not align were checked against the NCBI nr, and a large fraction was found to be prokaryotic rDNA.</p><p><i>Annotation</i>. Protein-coding genes were predicted with a pipeline that combines ESTs, homology and <i>de novo</i> prediction methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Goodstein, D.M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2321">23</a></sup>. Three-hundred twenty-nine million 108-bp-long single-ended Illumina reads from ten libraries of <i>C. grandiflora</i> cDNAs were assembled with tophat 1.3.0 and cufflinks 1.0.3, aligned to the <i>Capsella</i> genome and assembled with PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2331">24</a></sup> (alignments required 95% identity and 50% length), generating 28,322 EST assemblies with a median length of 1,426 bp. These were aligned to the <i>Capsella</i> genome (requiring 95% sequence identity and 50% coverage of the input sequence) and further assembled with PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2339">24</a></sup> to generate 27,399 EST assemblies with a median length of 1,432 bp. Predicted protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis</i> (v. TAIR10), papaya (ASGPB v0.4 Dec2) and grapevine (Genoscope 12 × 05/10/10) to the softmasked <i>Capsella</i> v1.0 assembly with gapped blastx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2349">25</a></sup> were aligned to generate putative protein-coding gene loci from regions with EST assemblies, protein homology or both, extending to include overlap where necessary. Gene predictions were generated from putative loci with FGenesH+<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Solovyev, V., Kosarev, P., Seledsov, I. &amp; Vorobyev, D. Automatic annotation of eukaryotic genes, pseudogenes and promoters. Genome Biol. 7 (suppl. 1), S10.1–S10.12 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2353">26</a></sup>, exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Slater, G.S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2358">27</a></sup> (with setting –model protein2genome) and GenomeScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yeh, R.F., Lim, L.P. &amp; Burge, C.B. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803–816 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2362">28</a></sup>. The gene prediction at each locus with the highest amount of support from EST assemblies and protein homology was chosen to be improved using evidence from the EST assemblies with a second round of PASA. Gene models with homology to repeats were removed. This produced an annotation at each of 26,521 protein-coding loci, with 1,926 alternative splice forms predicted to produce a total of 28,447 transcripts.</p><p><i>Whole-genome alignment</i>. Two approaches were used to conduct whole-genome alignments and identify orthologous gene positions. First, CoGe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2371">29</a></sup> was used, using the quota align algorithm in Synmap, to identify orthologous gene positions across the genomes of <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>S. parvula</i>.</p><p>Additionally, to gain more comprehensive synteny information for gene-poor regions, whole-genome orthologous alignments were generated for <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata.</i> To generate three-species whole-genome alignments, LASTZ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Harris, R.S. Improved Pairwise Alignment of Genomic DNA. PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2399">30</a></sup> alignments and orthologous chaining<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kent, W.J., Baertsch, R., Hinrichs, A., Miller, W. &amp; Haussler, D. Evolution's cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 100, 11484–11489 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2403">31</a></sup> were conducted using <i>Capsella</i> as the reference, retaining only a single chain per species per region. Version 0.62-1 of the circos software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2411">32</a></sup> was used to create the circular plots. For comparative mapping in the circos plots, orthologous chains were filtered to a minimum length of 100 kb.</p><p><i>RNA extraction, sequencing and read mapping</i>. Total RNA was harvested from mixed flower buds flash frozen in liquid nitrogen from five <i>C. grandiflora</i> genotypes from Greece and six <i>C. rubella</i> genotypes sampled from different geographic locations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 2</a>) using the RNAeasy plant mini kit (Qiagen) with minor modifications to obtain the required yield (<span class="stix">∼</span>5 μg) for RNA sequencing. After extraction, mRNA isolation, library preparation and paired-end 38-bp read sequencing were conducted on three flow cells of an Illumina Genome Analyzer (GAII) at the Centre for Analysis of Genome Evolution and Function (CAGEF) at the University of Toronto.</p><p>To compare our expression patterns in <i>Capsella</i> with those in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, we prepared duplicate RNA from stage-12 floral buds of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Col-0 reference accession) and <i>A. lyrata</i> (accession MN47 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2443">19</a>)). Barcoded RNA-seq libraries were prepared from each sample with an adaptation of the standard Illumina mRNA-seq method, and single-end sequencing (one flowcell lane worth) was performed to generate 78-bp reads on an Illumina Genome Analyzer (GAII). Methods for plant growth, floral bud collection, RNA extraction, library construction and sequencing for the <i>Arabidopsis</i> samples are as reported in Gan <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gan, X. et al. Multiple reference genomes and transcriptomes for Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 477, 419–423 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2454">33</a></sup>.</p><p>We used TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2461">34</a></sup> to map sequence reads to the reference genome of <i>C. rubella</i>. <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> RNA sequence reads were mapped to their respective reference genomes using the same parameters described above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genomic resequencing and analysis.</h3><p>Genomic extraction of leaf material was conducted for two <i>C. grandiflora</i> accessions and three <i>C. rubella</i> accessions, as well as one accession of <i>N. paniculata</i>, using a modified CTAB protocol; 108-bp paired-end genomic sequencing was conducted at the Genome Quebec Innovation Centre, and 150-bp sequencing was performed at the Max Planck Institute for Developmental Biology. To infer the ancestral state of segregating polymorphisms in <i>Capsella</i>, we mapped <i>Neslia</i> Illumina reads onto the <i>Capsella</i> reference genome using Stampy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lunter, G. &amp; Goodson, M. Stampy: a statistical algorithm for sensitive and fast mapping of Illumina sequence reads. Genome Res. 21, 936–939 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2501">35</a></sup>, which is optimized for mapping divergent sequences. Additionally, we conducted 5 kb–insert mate-pair sequencing of one of the <i>C. grandiflora</i> accessions and the <i>N. paniculata</i> accession and produced Illumina-only <i>de novo</i> assemblies as described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Transposable element annotation.</h3><p>The TEdenovo pipeline from the REPET v2.0 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS ONE 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2525">36</a></sup> was used for <i>de novo</i> identification of repeated sequences in the following genomes: <i>A. thaliana</i> (ecotypes Col, Ler, Kro, Bur and C24 from the 1001 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genomes project; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec7">URLs</a>), <i>A. lyrata</i>, <i>A. alpina</i> (courtesy of G. Coupland), <i>Brassica rapa</i>, <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>Eutrema halophila</i> and <i>S. parvula</i>. The selected sequences from all species were combined into the Brassicaceae repeat library, to which we also appended the <i>A. thaliana</i> repeat library from the Repbase database. TEannot from the REPET v2.0 package was run against the <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> genomes using the Brassicaceae library.</p><p>LTR retrotransposons were identified <i>de novo</i> for each genome using LTRharvest from the Genome Tools v1.3.9 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2579">37</a></sup> with default parameters. MUSCLE v3.8.31 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2582">38</a>) was used to align LTRs from annotated elements, alignment ends were trimmed at each end to have three consecutive matching nucleotides, and the Kimura two-parameter distance was calculated for each alignment using the EMBOSS v6.4.0 dismat function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2586">39</a></sup>. Insertion time was then calculated using the methods described in Hu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2594">19</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">FISH and comparative chromosome painting.</h3><p>Cytogenetic analyses were conducted using meiotic chromosomes at the stage of pachytene or diakinesis prepared from young flower buds. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Figure 8</a> for details of rDNA and telomere repeat probes and <i>A. thaliana</i> BAC contigs used as chromosome-specific probes for comparative chromosome painting in <i>C. rubella</i>. All DNA probes were labeled with biotin–deoxyuridine triphosphate (dUTP), digoxigenin-dUTP or Cy3-dUTP by nick translation and hybridized to suitable chromosome spreads. Fluorescence signals were analyzed with an Olympus BX-61 epifluorescence microscope and a CoolCube camera (MetaSystems) and pseudocolored using Adobe Photoshop CS2 software (Adobe Systems). See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#MOESM29">Supplementary Figure 8</a> and ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mandáková, T. Analysis of plant meiotic chromosomes by chromosome painting. Methods Mol. Biol 990, 13–24 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2669#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d47183e2617">40</a> for a detailed description of all protocols used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">URLs.</h3><p>JGI sequencing protocols, <a href="http://www.jgi.doe.gov/sequencing/protocols/prots_production.html">http://www.jgi.doe.gov/sequencing/protocols/prots_production.html</a>; 1001 <i>Arabidopsis</i> Genomes project, <a href="http://www.1001genomes.org/">http://www.1001genomes.org/</a>; PHYTOZOME portal, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/capsella.php">http://www.phytozome.net/capsella.php</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Accession codes.</h3><p>The assembly and annotation (Entrez BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA13878">PRJNA13878</a>) are available from GenBank (accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=ANNY00000000">ANNY00000000</a>) and from the JGI PHYTOZOME portal (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2669#Sec7">URLs</a>). RNA-seq data sets are available from GenBank (GEO SuperSeries <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE45687">GSE45687</a> and SRA accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=PRJNA194469">PRJNA194469</a>). Seeds from the reference <i>C. rubella</i> strain Monte Gargano are available from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Biological Resource Center (ABRC) under accession number <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/servlets/TairObject?type=germplasm&amp;id=3510598696">CS22697</a> and the Nottingham <i>Arabidopsis</i> Stock Centre (NASC) under accession number <a href="http://arabidopsis.info/StockInfo?NASC_id=9609">N9609</a>.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0959-437X%2800%2900254-9" aria-label="View reference 4">Article</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 4 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Breeding%20systems%20and%20genome%20evolution&amp;journal=Curr.%20Opin.%20Genet.%20Dev.&amp;volume=11&amp;pages=685-690&amp;publication_year=2001&amp;author=Charlesworth%2CD&amp;author=Wright%2CSI">

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="M.                         <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Stephen I+Wright" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Stephen I+Wright%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>B.S.G., M.N., J. Schmutz, T.S., D.R., D.W. and S.I.W. conceived and designed the study. Y.-L.G., K.M.H., D.K., J. Steffen and T.S. prepared samples for sequencing. J.J. and J. Schmutz conducted <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>C. rubella</i>. Y.-L.G., S.R.H., S.P., D.R. and S.S. performed genome annotation. J.G. led the data collection of BAC-end and clone sequencing. P.A., K.M.H., T.T.H., T.S. and S.I.W. conducted genetic mapping analysis. J.A.Å., Y.-L.G., D.K., F.M., H.Q. and W.W. performed the transposon analysis. Y.B., G.C., J.S.E., K.M.H., L.K.N., K.S., T.S. and S.I.W. conducted population genetic analysis. R.M.C., J. Steffen, L.M.S., K.M.H. and A.E.P. conducted the RNA sequence and expression analysis. M.B. and A.E.P. conducted <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>N. paniculata</i> and whole-genome alignments. J.C. led the <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>C. grandiflora</i>. A.E.P., S.T. and E.V. conducted comparative genomic analysis. T.M. and M.A.L. conducted FISH and chromosome painting. B.S.G., M.N., T.S., D.W. and S.I.W. wrote the paper with contributions from all authors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2669/email/correspondent/c1/new">Detlef Weigel</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2669/email/correspondent/c2/new">Stephen I Wright</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>
